        1. Promulgation of the Basic Law and Religious Provisions, 1990 (Basic Law 1990)

          In April 1990, the Third Session of the Seventh National People‚Äôs Congress promulgated the Basic Law, which included the following provisions related to religion:

          (1)Âü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÈ†í‰ΩàËàáÂÆóÊïôÊ¢ùÊ¨æ 1990ÔºàÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ï1990Ôºâ

          1990Âπ¥4ÊúàÔºå‰∏ÉÂ±ÜÂÖ®Âúã‰∫∫Â§ß‰∏âÊ¨°ÊúÉË≠∞È†í‰ΩàÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÔºåÂÖ∂‰∏≠Ê∂âÂèäÂÆóÊïôÁöÑÊ¢ùÊ¨æÂ¶Ç‰∏ãÔºö

          Á¨¨‰∏âÁ´†„ÄåÂ±ÖÊ∞ëÁöÑÂü∫Êú¨Ê¨äÂà©ÂíåÁæ©Âãô„Äç

          Chapter III: ‚ÄúFundamental Rights and Duties of Residents‚Äù

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÂ±ÖÊ∞ëÊúâ‰ø°‰ª∞ÁöÑËá™Áî±„ÄÇÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂ±ÖÊ∞ëÊúâÂÆóÊïô‰ø°‰ª∞ÁöÑËá™Áî±ÔºåÊúâÂÖ¨ÈñãÂÇ≥ÊïôÂíåËàâË°å„ÄÅÂèÉÂä†ÂÆóÊïôÊ¥ªÂãïÁöÑËá™Áî±„ÄÇÔºàÁ¨¨32Ê¢ùÔºâ

          Hong Kong residents shall have freedom of belief. Hong Kong residents shall enjoy the freedom of religious belief, the freedom to preach publicly, and the freedom to conduct and participate in religious activities. (Article 32)

          ÂêÑÈ°ûÈô¢Ê†°ÂùáÂèØ‰øùÁïôÂÖ∂Ëá™‰∏ªÊÄß‰∏¶‰∫´ÊúâÂ≠∏Ë°ìËá™Áî±ÔºåÂèØÁπºÁ∫åÂæûÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄ‰ª•Â§ñÊãõËÅòÊïôËÅ∑Âì°ÂíåÈÅ∏Áî®ÊïôÊùê„ÄÇ

          ÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÊâÄËæ¶ÁöÑÂ≠∏Ê†°ÂèØÁπºÁ∫åÊèê‰æõÂÆóÊïôÊïôËÇ≤ÔºåÂåÖÊã¨ÈñãË®≠ÂÆóÊïôË™≤Á®ã„ÄÇÂ≠∏Áîü‰∫´ÊúâÈÅ∏ÊìáÈô¢Ê†°ÂíåÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄ‰ª•Â§ñÊ±ÇÂ≠∏ÁöÑËá™Áî±„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨137Ê¢ùÔºâ

          All types of educational institutions may retain their autonomy and enjoy academic freedom. They may continue to recruit teaching staff and select educational materials from outside the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region.

          Schools run by religious organizations may continue to provide religious education, including offering religious courses. Students shall have the freedom to choose their educational institutions and to pursue education outside the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region. (Article 137)

          Chapter VI: ‚ÄúEducation, Science, Culture, Sports, Religion, Labor, and Social Services‚Äù

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÊîøÂ∫ú‰∏çÈôêÂà∂ÂÆóÊïô‰ø°‰ª∞Ëá™Áî±Ôºå‰∏çÂπ≤È†êÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÁöÑÂÖßÈÉ®‰∫ãÂãôÔºå‰∏çÈôêÂà∂ËàáÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÊ≥ïÂæãÊ≤íÊúâÊäµËß∏ÁöÑÂÆóÊïôÊ¥ªÂãï„ÄÇ

          ÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπî‰æùÊ≥ï‰∫´ÊúâË≤°Áî¢ÁöÑÂèñÂæó„ÄÅ‰ΩøÁî®„ÄÅËôïÁΩÆ„ÄÅÁπºÊâø‰ª•ÂèäÊé•ÂèóË≥áÂä©ÁöÑÊ¨äÂà©„ÄÇË≤°Áî¢ÊñπÈù¢ÁöÑÂéüÊúâÊ¨äÁõä‰ªç‰∫à‰øùÊåÅÂíå‰øùË≠∑„ÄÇ

          ÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂèØÊåâÂéüÊúâËæ¶Ê≥ïÁπºÁ∫åËààËæ¶ÂÆóÊïôÈô¢Ê†°„ÄÅÂÖ∂‰ªñÂ≠∏Ê†°„ÄÅÈÜ´Èô¢ÂíåÁ¶èÂà©Ê©üÊßã‰ª•ÂèäÊèê‰æõÂÖ∂‰ªñÁ§æÊúÉÊúçÂãô„ÄÇ

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁöÑÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂíåÊïôÂæíÂèØËàáÂÖ∂‰ªñÂú∞ÊñπÁöÑÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂíåÊïôÂæí‰øùÊåÅÂíåÁôºÂ±ïÈóú‰øÇ„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨141Ê¢ùÔºâ

          The Government of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region shall not restrict the freedom of religious belief, interfere in the internal affairs of religious organizations, or limit religious activities that are not in contravention of the laws of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region.

          Religious organizations shall, in accordance with the law, enjoy the rights to acquire, use, dispose of, and inherit property, as well as to receive donations. Their original property rights and interests shall be maintained and protected.

          Religious organizations may, in accordance with past practices, continue to operate religious schools, other educational institutions, hospitals, and welfare institutions, and provide other social services.

          Religious organizations and believers in the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region may maintain and develop their relations with religious organizations and believers in other places. (Article 141)

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁöÑÊïôËÇ≤„ÄÅÁßëÂ≠∏„ÄÅÊäÄË°ì„ÄÅÊñáÂåñ„ÄÅËóùË°ì„ÄÅÈ´îËÇ≤„ÄÅÂ∞àÊ•≠„ÄÅÈÜ´ÁôÇË°õÁîü„ÄÅÂãûÂ∑•„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÂ∑•‰ΩúÁ≠âÊñπÈù¢ÁöÑÊ∞ëÈñìÂúòÈ´îÂíåÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂêåÂÖßÂú∞Áõ∏ÊáâÁöÑÂúòÈ´îÂíåÁµÑÁπîÁöÑÈóú‰øÇÔºåÊáâ‰ª•‰∫í‰∏çÈö∏Â±¨„ÄÅ‰∫í‰∏çÂπ≤Ê∂âÂíå‰∫íÁõ∏Â∞äÈáçÁöÑÂéüÂâáÁÇ∫Âü∫Á§é„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨148Ê¢ùÔºâ

          The relationship between non-governmental organizations and religious organizations in the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region involved in education, science, technology, culture, arts, sports, professions, healthcare, labor, social welfare, and social work, and their counterparts in the Mainland, shall be based on the principles of non-subordination, non-interference, and mutual respect. (Article 148)

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁöÑÊïôËÇ≤„ÄÅÁßëÂ≠∏„ÄÅÊäÄË°ì„ÄÅÊñáÂåñ„ÄÅËóùË°ì„ÄÅÈ´îËÇ≤„ÄÅÂ∞àÊ•≠„ÄÅÈÜ´ÁôÇË°õÁîü„ÄÅÂãûÂ∑•„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÂ∑•‰ΩúÁ≠âÊñπÈù¢ÁöÑÊ∞ëÈñìÂúòÈ´îÂíåÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂèØÂêå‰∏ñÁïåÂêÑÂúã„ÄÅÂêÑÂú∞ÂçÄÂèäÂúãÈöõÁöÑÊúâÈóúÂúòÈ´îÂíåÁµÑÁπî‰øùÊåÅÂíåÁôºÂ±ïÈóú‰øÇÔºåÂêÑË©≤ÂúòÈ´îÂíåÁµÑÁπîÂèØÊ†πÊìöÈúÄË¶ÅÂÜ†Áî®„Äå‰∏≠ÂúãÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄçÁöÑÂêçÁæ©ÔºåÂèÉËàáÊúâÈóúÊ¥ªÂãï„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨149Ê¢ù Ôºâ

          Non-governmental organizations and religious organizations in the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region involved in education, science, technology, culture, arts, sports, professions, healthcare, labor, social welfare, and social work may maintain and develop relations with relevant organizations and groups in other countries, regions, and international communities. These organizations and groups may, as necessary, use the name ‚ÄúHong Kong, China‚Äù to participate in relevant activities. (Article 149)

          From the finalized provisions of the Basic Law concerning religion, it is evident that the expectations of Hong Kong‚Äôs religious community to maintain the status quo were largely realized. The Chinese concept of the separation of church and state did not appear in the finalized text. The arrangements made by China and the United Kingdom for Hong Kong‚Äôs sovereignty transfer, as noted by Lui Tai-lok, reflect a ‚Äúfreezing Hong Kong‚Äù mindset‚Äîpreserving Hong Kong‚Äôs original model, maintaining the status quo, unchanged for fifty years (Lui 2002: 462). Viewed in this light, the efforts of Hong Kong‚Äôs Christian community before 1997 to safeguard religious freedom and rights, particularly during the discussions and debates in the drafting process of the Basic Law, were motivated by a desire to maintain the status quo. These efforts ultimately resulted in the transplantation of the Basic Law‚Äôs provisions to post-1997 Hong Kong.

          ÂæûÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÊ∂âÂèäÂÆóÊïôÊ¢ùÊñáÁöÑÂÆöÁ®øÂèØË¶ãÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂÆóÊïôÁïåÁà≠ÂèñÁ∂≠ÊåÅÁèæÁãÄÁöÑÊúüÊúõÔºåÂ§ßÊäµÂú®Áõ∏ÈóúÊ¢ùÊ¨æ‰∏≠ËêΩÂØ¶ÔºåËÄå‰∏≠ÂúãÁâàÊú¨ÁöÑÊîøÊïôÂàÜÈõ¢Ê¶ÇÂøµ‰πü‰∏¶Êú™Ë¶ãÊñºÂÆöÁ®ø„ÄÇ‰∏≠Ëã±ÂÖ©ÂúãÂ∞±È¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∏ªÊ¨äÁßª‰∫§ÊâÄ‰ΩúÁöÑÂÆâÊéíÔºåË™†Â¶ÇÂëÇÂ§ßÊ®ÇÊåáÂá∫Ôºå‰πÉÂá∫Êñº‰∏ÄÁ®Æ„ÄåÊÄ•ÂáçÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄçÁöÑÊÄùÁ∂≠ÔºåÂç≥ÊääÂéüÊúâÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁöÑÊ®°Âºè„ÄåÂáùÂõ∫Ëµ∑‰æÜ„ÄçÔºåÁ∂≠ÊåÅÁèæÁãÄÔºå‰∫îÂçÅÂπ¥‰∏çËÆäÔºàÂëÇÂ§ßÊ®Ç2002Ôºö462Ôºâ„ÄÇÈÄôÊ®£Áúã‰æÜÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂü∫Áù£Êïô‰∫∫Â£´Âú®‰πù‰∏ÉÂâçÂ∞çÂÆóÊïôËá™Áî±ÂèäÊ¨äÁõäÁöÑÁà≠ÂèñÔºåÁâπÂà•Âú®Âü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïËµ∑ËçâÈÅéÁ®ãÁöÑË®éË´ñÂèäËæØË´ñÔºå‰πüÊòØÂá∫ÊñºÈÄôÁ®ÆÁ∂≠ÊåÅÁèæÁãÄÁöÑËÄÉÊÖÆÔºåÊúÄÂæåÈÄöÈÅéÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÂæó‰ª•ÁßªÊ§çËá≥‰πù‰∏ÉÂæåÁöÑÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ

          1. Hong Kong Christianity Under the Post-Transition Chinese Framework (Hing Fuk-tsang 2018: 208, 214)

            In 1985, the composition of the Basic Law Drafting and Consultative Committees followed the Chinese government‚Äôs model of representatives from various sectors, as seen in the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference. During the post-transition period, religious representatives were allocated seats within Chinese frameworks, forming a ‚Äúreligious representative‚Äù mechanism:

            Due to the Sino-British dispute over Governor Chris Patten‚Äôs political reforms, the Chinese government established the ‚ÄúHong Kong Affairs Advisors‚Äù in 1992 to ensure a smooth transition and maintain prosperity and stability. The term lasted until June 30, 1997. A total of 187 advisors were appointed in four batches between March 1992, including Anglican Bishop Peter Kwong of the Diocese of Hong Kong and Macau, who was appointed as a Christian Affairs Advisor.

            On January 26, 1996, the Preparatory Committee for the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region was established based on the decision made at the Third Session of the Seventh National People‚Äôs Congress on April 4, 1990. The committee comprised 150 members, 94 from Hong Kong and 56 from Mainland China. Bishop Peter Kwong was appointed as a member of the Preparatory Committee.

            In 1996, the Election Committee for the First Government of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region was formed by the Preparatory Committee. It was responsible for selecting the first Chief Executive and the Provisional Legislative Council. The Christian representatives elected to the committee were Bishop Peter Kwong, Rev. Philip Tse (Chairman of the Hong Kong Christian Council), and Ms. Yuk Tak-fun (General Secretary of the Hong Kong YWCA).

            After 1997, the Beijing government strengthened its engagement with religious figures. The most notable measure was appointing religious leaders as delegates to the National Committee of the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference (CPPCC) as ‚ÄúSpecially Invited Hong Kong Members.‚Äù For Christianity, Bishop Peter Kwong was appointed to the 9th and 10th CPPCC in 1998 and 2003, respectively. After Bishop Paul Kwong succeeded him as the Archbishop and Primate of the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui in 2007, he was appointed to the 12th and 13th CPPCC in 2013 and 2018. In 2020, Archbishop Andrew Chan, who succeeded Bishop Paul Kwong, was appointed to the 14th CPPCC in 2023.

            ‚Äî

            1. Government‚Äôs First Funding for Gospel-Based Drug Rehabilitation, 1998 (Lee 2001: 73‚Äì79; Christian Times 1998)

              Historically, the government did not recognize the effectiveness of gospel-based drug rehabilitation programs. However, following the second drug summit in May 1996, chaired by Governor Chris Patten, the government decided to include non-governmental organizations (NGOs) in its funding scope, contingent on an evaluation of their rehabilitation programs. In January 1997, the Social Welfare Department commissioned Professor Patrick Chan Kai-nai of the Chinese University of Hong Kong‚Äôs Faculty of Medicine to conduct a relevant study.

              By December 1997, five organizations were confirmed to meet the Social Welfare Department‚Äôs criteria and were deemed eligible for funding: Barnabas Charitable Services Association, Christian New Being Fellowship, Ling Oi Youth Centre (Finnish Evangelical Lutheran Mission), Operation Dawn‚Äôs Island Centre, and St. Stephen‚Äôs Society.

              In March 1998, the Social Welfare Department began funding four NGOs providing voluntary residential drug rehabilitation programs: Barnabas Charitable Services Association, Ling Oi Youth Centre, Operation Dawn, and Christian New Being Fellowship (St. Stephen‚Äôs Society declined funding as it had secured alternative financial support). These programs varied in length and content but were all faith-based, emphasizing psychosocial recovery counseling and offering follow-up support services. For the 1998‚Äì99 fiscal year, the government allocated over HK$16 million to these programs.

              1996Âπ¥ÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁ¨¨‰∏ÄÂ±ÜÊîøÂ∫úÊé®ÈÅ∏ÂßîÂì°ÊúÉÁî±È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁ±åÂÇôÂßîÂì°ÊúÉÁ±åÁµÑËÄåÊàêÔºåË≤†Ë≤¨ÈÅ∏Âá∫È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁ¨¨‰∏ÄÂ±ÜË°åÊîøÈï∑ÂÆòÂèäËá®ÊôÇÁ´ãÊ≥ïÊúÉ„ÄÇÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁïåÊé®Âßî‰ª£Ë°®Áî±ÈÑ∫Âª£ÂÇëÔºàËÅñÂÖ¨ÊúÉÊ∏ØÊæ≥ÊïôÂçÄ‰∏ªÊïôÔºâ„ÄÅËñõÁ£êÂü∫ÔºàÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÂçîÈÄ≤ÊúÉ‰∏ªÂ∏≠ÔºâÂèäÈÉÅÂæ∑Ëä¨ÔºàÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÂ•≥ÈùíÂπ¥ÊúÉÁ∏ΩÂππ‰∫ãÔºâÁï∂ÈÅ∏„ÄÇ

              1. Buddha‚Äôs Birthday Recognized as a Public Holiday for the First Time, 1999 (Buddhist Door, 2024)

                On May 22, 1999, Buddha‚Äôs Birthday was officially celebrated as a public holiday in Hong Kong for the first time. The Hong Kong Buddhist Association made a special request to the Chinese government and the Buddhist Association of China to bring the Buddha‚Äôs tooth relic, a national treasure from Lingguang Temple in Beijing, to Hong Kong. On that day, the Hong Kong Buddhist community held a grand celebration and veneration ceremony at the Hong Kong Coliseum, officiated by Elder Sik Kok-kwong. Prominent attendees included Zhao Puchu, Vice Chairman of the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference and President of the Buddhist Association of China, and Tung Chee-hwa, Chief Executive of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region (HKSAR).

                As early as 1961, Sik Kok-kwong of the Hong Kong Buddhist Association had petitioned the Hong Kong government to recognize Buddha‚Äôs Birthday as a public holiday. The Director of Home Affairs expressed willingness to assist. The association launched a ‚Äú100,000 Signatures Campaign‚Äù to appeal to the government. Two years later, the association collected 120,000 signatures, which Sik Kok-kwong submitted to the Director of Home Affairs for forwarding to the Governor. While the government promised to consider the request, no decision was made for years.

                In 1985, Sik Kok-kwong, appointed as a member of the Basic Law Drafting Committee, raised the issue again during the committee‚Äôs discussions.

                In 1998, he renewed his appeal to the HKSAR government, submitting an official request, Application for Implementing Buddha‚Äôs Birthday as a Public Holiday in 1999, and a related petition to the Xinhua News Agency Hong Kong Branch. The petition emphasized that ‚ÄúHong Kong, under British rule for many years, had favored Western religious holidays, while holidays for religions predominantly followed by Chinese residents were largely absent, leading to a sense of discrimination. For equality, Buddha‚Äôs Birthday should be designated as a public holiday.‚Äù

                In 1998, the HKSAR government submitted the Holidays (Amendment) Ordinance 1998. A government spokesperson stated: ‚ÄúThe primary purpose of the ordinance is to designate Labor Day (May 1) and Buddha‚Äôs Birthday (the eighth day of the fourth lunar month) as public holidays starting in 1999, replacing the two public holidays observed in 1997 and 1998‚ÄîVictory Day of the Anti-Japanese War (the third Monday of August) and the day following National Day (October 2).‚Äù

                1. Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Ordinance, Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation Ordinance, and Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee Ordinance, 1999 (Press Release, 1999)

                  Following the establishment ceremony of the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui (Hong Kong Anglican Church Province) on October 25, 1998, the Secretary for Home Affairs signed a notice at the request of the church to enact the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Ordinance (Cap. 1157), the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee Ordinance (Cap. 1158), and the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation Ordinance (Cap. 1159). These ordinances came into effect on February 26, 1999, and were gazetted the same day.

                  The Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Ordinance established the ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Province‚Äù as a corporate body under the name ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui‚Äù and provided provisions regarding its composition, powers, responsibilities, and related matters.

                  The Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation Ordinance provided for the continuation of the corporation formerly known as the ‚ÄúBishop of Victoria‚Äù under the name ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation.‚Äù It outlined the foundation‚Äôs composition, powers, and responsibilities while repealing the Bishop of Victoria Incorporation Ordinance.

                  The Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee Ordinance ensured the continuation of the corporation formerly known as the ‚ÄúDiocesan Trusts Committee‚Äù under the name ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee.‚Äù It specified the committee‚Äôs composition, powers, and responsibilities, while repealing the Diocesan Trusts Committee Incorporation Ordinance.

                  1. The ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù Debate, 1995‚Äì1996 (Institute of Sino-Christian Studies, 1997; Chan Shing-king, 1998; Lee 2010: 53‚Äì62)

                    From September 1995 to May 1996, a ten-month-long debate on ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù unfolded in the weekly publication Christian Times. Eight individuals participated in the discussion: four from Hong Kong, three from Mainland China, and one from Taiwan. The Hong Kong contributors represented both evangelical and ecumenical traditions, while the Mainland contributors included academics and Christian researchers. The debate centered on how to understand the phenomenon of ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù in China and how Hong Kong‚Äôs theological community evaluated it. Central to the discussion were issues of orthodox theological authority and the representation of theology, particularly in relation to debates with Mainland scholars.

                    The debate was initiated by Lo Ping-cheung of the Department of Religion and Philosophy at Hong Kong Baptist University, who published the article ‚ÄúChina‚Äôs Apollos and the 1997 Crisis for Hong Kong Theology.‚Äù Lo likened the interest in Christian culture among Mainland Chinese intellectuals and scholars to ‚Äúthe Macedonian Call.‚Äù He noted that those responding to this call were not the Mainland churches or seminaries but scholars engaged in Christian studies at universities, whom he referred to as ‚ÄúChina‚Äôs Apollos.‚Äù He specifically highlighted figures such as Liu Xiaofeng (working at Tao Fong Shan Christian Centre and the Institute of Chinese Studies at The Chinese University of Hong Kong), He Guanghu, Zhuo Xinping, and Tang Yi (from the Chinese Academy of Social Sciences), as well as Zhao Dunhua (Peking University). Lo urged Hong Kong‚Äôs theological community to broaden its perspective beyond Hong Kong and focus on China as a whole. Failure to do so, he argued, would risk losing theological influence not only in China but also in Hong Kong itself, relegating Hong Kong theology to a passive role (Lo 1995).

                    In response, Leung Ka-lun of the Alliance Bible Seminary wrote ‚ÄúIs This Another Debt We Owe?‚Äù He argued that the traditional church and theological community must acknowledge that they no longer hold exclusive representational authority over Christianity or the right to limit others‚Äô voices. While accepting that losing some authority was inevitable, Leung warned against over-praising the so-called ‚ÄúChina‚Äôs Apollos‚Äù and emphasized the need to critically assess whether their non-orthodox approaches could lead to misunderstandings of Christianity. At the same time, Leung stressed that Hong Kong‚Äôs churches and theological community should not be burdened with the overwhelming responsibility of shaping the future of both Hong Kong and Chinese culture (Leung 1995a).

                    Kwok Tak-wai of the Divinity School of Chung Chi College responded by asserting that Hong Kong‚Äôs theological community neither could nor should aim to be the theological spokesperson for China. He also argued that the influence of Western theologians, who were often unfamiliar with the local context but still ‚Äúpreached‚Äù in Hong Kong, posed a greater threat than ‚ÄúChina‚Äôs Apollos‚Äù (Kwok 1995).

                    Lee King-heung, former president of the Christian Study Centre on Chinese Religion and Culture, elaborated on the concept of ‚Äútheological authority.‚Äù He argued that true theological authority derives from understanding God‚Äôs teachings and questioned whether ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù could fully represent orthodox theology, given their focus on translating and introducing foreign theological ideas (Lee 1995).

                    Responses from Mainland contributors included Li Qiuling (Renmin University of China), Zhang Xianyong (Nanjing Union Theological Seminary), and Wu Bofan (Chinese Academy of Social Sciences), writing under the pseudonym ‚ÄúBo Fan.‚Äù Li criticized the self-proclaimed orthodoxy of some in Hong Kong‚Äôs theological community, asserting that no denomination or individual could claim an exclusive understanding of Christian faith (Li 1995). Similarly, Bo Fan rejected the principle of ‚Äúorthodoxy‚Äù proposed by Hong Kong theologians but noted that ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù approach theology as a personal ‚Äúconfession of faith,‚Äù with cultural rather than ecclesiastical dimensions (Bo Fan 1995). Zhang, with a background in church and theological education in China, distinguished between ‚Äúinsiders‚Äù and ‚Äúoutsiders,‚Äù asserting that ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù had no intention of competing for theological authority in Hong Kong (Zhang 1996).

                    Leung Ka-lun responded to Zhang, emphasizing that his concern was not about drawing boundaries around the church but about how ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù understood Christianity. He argued that Christianity is a historical faith and warned against reductionist approaches that risk fragmenting the understanding of Christianity (Leung 1996).

                    In Taiwan, Chen Ching-pao analyzed the debate through the lenses of ‚Äúecclesiastical theology‚Äù and ‚Äúhumanistic theology.‚Äù He argued that ecclesiastical theology, rooted in pastoral care and evangelism, lacked the capacity to address modern phenomena. Since ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù were not part of the pastoral system or converted through evangelism, their discourse primarily drew from cultural and intellectual contexts (Chen 1996).

                    In 1997, the Institute of Sino-Christian Studies compiled the debate and subsequent discussions into a publication titled Cultural Christians: Phenomena and Controversies.

                    1. Christian Evangelism Provokes Buddhist Dissatisfaction, 1999 (Christian Times, 1999)

                      On May 22, 1999, the first public holiday for Buddha‚Äôs Birthday in Hong Kong, the Asia for Christ Mission Association held an event titled ‚ÄúThe First Priority: Evangelism Techniques Training Day.‚Äù The promotional materials claimed, ‚ÄúAt a time when many are seeking religious solace, Satan seizes the opportunity to steal souls.‚Äù They also noted that Buddha‚Äôs Birthday fell on the eve of Pentecost, framing it as a ‚Äúspiritual battle.‚Äù The event included activities such as ‚Äúrepentance for idol worship among Chinese people‚Äù and training on ‚Äúhow to evangelize to Buddhists.‚Äù

                      This publicity sparked dissatisfaction among some Buddhists, who initially planned to issue a public response. However, with mediation by a third party, the matter was resolved amicably and handled with minimal publicity.

                      Âü∫Áù£Êïô‰∏≠ÂúãÂÆóÊïôÊñáÂåñÁ†îÁ©∂Á§æÂâçÁ§æÈï∑ÊùéÊôØÈõÑÔºå‰∏ªË¶ÅÈó°Ëø∞‰ªñÂ∞ç„ÄåÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÁôºË®ÄÊ¨ä„ÄçÁöÑÁúãÊ≥ï„ÄÇ‰ªñË™çÁÇ∫„ÄåÊâÄÁôº‰πãË®ÄÊòØÂæûÁ•ûËÄå‰æÜÁöÑÂ≠∏Âïè„ÄÇÂ∞±ÊòØ„ÄåÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÁôºË®Ä‰∫∫„Äç„ÄÇ‰ªñË™çÁÇ∫Ôºå‰∏≠ÂúãÁöÑ„ÄåÊñáÂåñÂü∫Áù£Âæí„Äç‰∏çË¶ãÂæóÈÉΩÂÖ®Èù¢ÁÇ∫Ê≠£Áµ±Á•ûÂ≠∏ÁôºË®ÄÔºå‰∏¶‰∏î‰ªñÂÄë‰∏ªË¶ÅÂæû‰∫ãÁøªË≠Ø‰ªãÁ¥πÂ§ñÂúãÁöÑÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÊÄùÊÉ≥ÔºàÊùéÊôØÈõÑ1995Ôºâ„ÄÇ

                      ‰∏äËø∞4‰ΩçÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ•ûÂ≠∏Áïå‰∫∫Â£´ÁöÑË®éË´ñÔºåÊé•ËëóÂºïËµ∑‰æÜËá™‰∏≠ÂúãÂæû‰∫ãÁù£ÊïôÁ†îÁ©∂ÁöÑÊùéÁßãÈõ∂Ôºà‰∏≠Âúã‰∫∫Ê∞ëÂ§ßÂ≠∏ÔºâÔºåÂèäÂçó‰∫¨ÈáëÈôµÂçîÂíåÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÁèæÁöÑÂºµË≥¢ÂãáÁöÑÂõûÊáâ„ÄÇÂè¶‰∏Ä‰Ωç‰ª•„ÄåÂçöÂá°„ÄçÁÇ∫Á≠ÜÂêçÁöÑÂê≥‰ºØÂá°Ôºà‰∏≠ÂúãÁ§æÊúÉÁßëÂ≠∏Èô¢‰∏ñÁïåÂÆóÊïôÁ†îÁ©∂ÊâÄÔºâ‰πüÊí∞Êñá„ÄÇÊùéÁßãÈõ∂Ë™çÁÇ∫ÔºåÊ≤íÊúâ‰ªª‰ΩïÊïôÊ¥æ„ÄÅ‰ªª‰ΩïÂÄã‰∫∫ÂèØ‰ª•ÂÆ£Á®±Ëá™Â∑±Â∞çÂü∫Áù£Êïô‰ø°‰ª∞ÁöÑÈ´îË™çÊòØÊ≠£Á¢∫ÁöÑÔºå‰ªñ‰∏çÊªøÈÉ®ÂàÜÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ•ûÂ≠∏Áïå‰ª•„ÄåÊ≠£Áµ±„ÄçËá™Â±ÖÔºàÊùéÁßãÈõ∂1995Ôºâ„ÄÇÂçöÂÆö‰πü‰∏çÊªøÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÁïåÊèêÂá∫ÁöÑ„ÄåÊ≠£Áµ±ÊÄß„ÄçÂéüÂâáÔºå‰ΩÜ‰ªñÂçªÊåáÂá∫„ÄåÊñáÂåñÂü∫Áù£Âæí„ÄçÁõ¥Êé•Âü∫Áù£‰∫ã‰ª∂ÊòØ‰∏ÄÈ´î„ÄåÂÄãÈ´îÁöÑË™ç‰ø°„ÄçÔºåÂÖ∑Êúâ„ÄåÊñáÂåñÊÄß„ÄçÂèàÈùû„ÄåÊïôÊúÉÊÄß„ÄçÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÁöÑÂê´Áæ©ÔºàÂçöÂá°1995Ôºâ„ÄÇËá≥ÊñºÂºµË≥¢ÂãáÔºåÁî±ÊñºÂÖ∑Êúâ‰∏≠ÂúãÊïôÊúÉÂèäÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÊïôËÇ≤ÁöÑËÉåÊôØÔºåÊïÖ‰ª•„ÄåÂúàÂÖß„ÄçÂèä„ÄåÂúàÂ§ñ„Äç‰ΩúÂõûÊáâÔºåÊåá„ÄåÊñáÂåñÂü∫Áù£Âæí„ÄçÂú®„ÄåÂúàÂ§ñ„ÄçÊ†πÊú¨Ê≤íÊúâ‰ªª‰ªñÁà≠Â•™È¶ôÊ∏ØÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÁôºË®ÄÊ¨äÁöÑ‰ºÅÂúñ„ÄÇ‰ªñÂèàÂÖÖÂàÜ‰∫ÜËß£„ÄåÂúàÂÖß„ÄçÁöÑÈôêÂà∂ÔºàÂºµË≥¢Âãá1996Ôºâ„ÄÇ

                      1. Sino-British Relations Post-Tiananmen Incident (Carroll 2007: 191‚Äì195, 198‚Äì203; Lau Chi-pang & Lau Shu-wing, 2019: 436‚Äì439, 443‚Äì447)

                        After the Tiananmen Incident in 1989, the United Kingdom altered its policy toward China, leading to a deterioration in Sino-British relations. Western nations adopted a containment strategy against the Chinese Communist Party (CCP), isolating China diplomatically. Meanwhile, the collapse of Communist regimes in the Soviet Union and Eastern Europe fueled Western beliefs in ‚Äúthe end of history,‚Äù where liberal democracy would triumph over Communism.

                        In April 1994, the UK Parliament‚Äôs Foreign Affairs Committee released a report explicitly stating that the British government did not believe the Beijing regime would necessarily remain in power until 1997. Following Tiananmen, the UK unilaterally suspended a planned meeting of the Sino-British Joint Liaison Group in July. In 1991, the UK enacted the Bill of Rights Ordinance, which nullified existing Hong Kong laws that conflicted with the ordinance and required future laws to comply with it. This drew strong criticism from Beijing.

                        Sino-British tensions also affected major infrastructure projects, such as the new Hong Kong airport. In October 1989, Governor David Wilson announced the ‚ÄúRose Garden Project,‚Äù proposing a new airport at Chek Lap Kok on Lantau Island. The existing Kai Tak Airport was overburdened, and the project aimed to lift Hong Kong‚Äôs post-Tiananmen malaise. However, the announcement was made without prior consultation with Beijing, raising concerns in China that the plan would deplete Hong Kong‚Äôs reserves, leaving the future HKSAR government burdened with debt. Negotiations ensued, as the UK recognized it needed China‚Äôs support to secure funding for the project beyond 1997. In September 1991, UK Prime Minister John Major visited Beijing, breaking the diplomatic freeze since Tiananmen. Both sides signed a memorandum of understanding: the UK committed to leaving HK$250 billion in reserves for the HKSAR government, while China agreed to support the airport project.

                        In April 1992, John Major appointed Christopher Patten as the last Governor of Hong Kong. In October, Patten proposed political reforms in his Policy Address, increasing directly elected seats in the Legislative Council from 18 to 20, expanding the voter base for functional constituencies, and lowering the voting age from 21 to 18. These changes significantly boosted democracy in the 1995 Legislative Council elections. Beijing, however, strongly opposed the reforms, calling them violations of the Sino-British Joint Declaration, the Basic Law, and mutual agreements. Seventeen rounds of talks between April and November 1993 failed to resolve the disputes.

                        Beijing subsequently decided that members of Hong Kong‚Äôs last Legislative Council, municipal councils, and district boards would not ‚Äútransition‚Äù past June 30, 1997. Instead, a Provisional Legislative Council was formed in Shenzhen in 1996.

                        ‚Äî

                        1. Hong Kong-China Relations from Tiananmen to 1997 (Carroll 2007: 197‚Äì198, 215; Chong Yiu-kwong & Tsui Ka-wing, 2014: 101‚Äì106)

                          In the early 1990s, Hong Kong could not escape the shadow of the Tiananmen Massacre. Public confidence plummeted, with widespread anxiety about the post-1997 future. Despite this, the first Legislative Council elections with direct voting were held in 1991. Pro-democracy candidates won 17 of the 18 directly elected seats and several functional constituencies, reflecting the ‚ÄúTiananmen effect.‚Äù

                          Meanwhile, the drafting of the Basic Law, which began in the mid-1980s, was completed in February 1990 and approved in April that year by the National People‚Äôs Congress (NPC). The CCP, concerned about Hong Kong becoming a ‚Äúbase of anti-Communist subversion,‚Äù added Article 23, mandating HKSAR legislation to prohibit acts such as treason, secession, sedition, subversion, and foreign political activities. Provisions were also added to limit Legislative Council powers.

                          On June 30, 1997, the Sino-British handover ceremony took place, and the HKSAR government was established on July 1. Democratic legislators, prematurely dismissed, vowed on the Legislative Council balcony to ‚Äúreturn.‚Äù The Provisional Legislative Council operated from January 1997 to April 1998, passing significant legislation, including amendments to the Public Order Ordinance and abolishing collective bargaining rights. Critics argue that post-1997 Hong Kong underwent ‚Äúrecolonization,‚Äù with Beijing replacing London as the sovereign power, raising concerns about whether ‚Äúone country, two systems‚Äù could sustain Hong Kong‚Äôs unique way of life.

                          ‚Äî

                          1. Deng Xiaoping‚Äôs Southern Tour and Shifts in U.S. Policy Toward China (Xu Cheng-en, 2023: 279‚Äì285; Carroll 2007: 210‚Äì211)

                            In 1992, Deng Xiaoping‚Äôs southern tour reaffirmed the policy of reform and opening up, advancing China‚Äôs economic transformation and improving Sino-U.S. relations.

                            Later that year, Bill Clinton was elected U.S. president, promoting the ‚ÄúDemocratic Enlargement‚Äù policy to replace Cold War dynamics with global integration. Under this framework, the U.S. and other Western nations shifted from containment to engagement with China, hoping economic reforms would lead to political liberalization. Congress leaned toward granting China most-favored-nation status and delinking trade policies from human rights issues. With U.S. support, China further integrated into global trade, culminating in its 2001 entry into the World Trade Organization (WTO).

                            The lifting of trade sanctions by Western countries spurred foreign investment, much of which flowed through Hong Kong into China, benefiting Hong Kong‚Äôs economy. This deepened economic interdependence between Hong Kong and the Mainland.

                            The 1992 U.S.‚ÄìHong Kong Policy Act allowed the U.S. to treat Hong Kong as a separate territory from China post-1997, provided it maintained ‚Äúsufficient autonomy.‚Äù

                            ‚Äî

                            1. Asian Financial Crisis After 1997 (Lui Tai-lok, 2002)

                              While political issues dominated pre-1997 concerns, post-1997 Hong Kong faced severe economic challenges, including the Asian Financial Crisis in late 1997. The crisis burst bubbles in Hong Kong‚Äôs stock and real estate markets, causing plummeting prices and widespread ‚Äúnegative equity.‚Äù Rising unemployment exacerbated social and economic problems.

                              Sociologist Lui Tai-lok observed that Hong Kong society, starting on July 1, 1997, experienced systemic failure. Institutional arrangements devised in the 1980s to preserve stability effectively collapsed in the face of the crisis.

Historical Period: Before 1900 (Focus on the decade from 1890 to 1900. As background, briefly mention significant past regulations and events, especially those that had a major impact on the situation during this decade.)

        1. Promulgation of the Basic Law and Religious Provisions, 1990 (Basic Law 1990)

          In April 1990, the Third Session of the Seventh National People‚Äôs Congress promulgated the Basic Law, which included the following provisions related to religion:

          (1)Âü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÈ†í‰ΩàËàáÂÆóÊïôÊ¢ùÊ¨æ 1990ÔºàÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ï1990Ôºâ

          Chapter III: ‚ÄúFundamental Rights and Duties of Residents‚Äù

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÂ±ÖÊ∞ëÊúâ‰ø°‰ª∞ÁöÑËá™Áî±„ÄÇÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂ±ÖÊ∞ëÊúâÂÆóÊïô‰ø°‰ª∞ÁöÑËá™Áî±ÔºåÊúâÂÖ¨ÈñãÂÇ≥ÊïôÂíåËàâË°å„ÄÅÂèÉÂä†ÂÆóÊïôÊ¥ªÂãïÁöÑËá™Áî±„ÄÇÔºàÁ¨¨32Ê¢ùÔºâ

          Hong Kong residents shall have freedom of belief. Hong Kong residents shall enjoy the freedom of religious belief, the freedom to preach publicly, and the freedom to conduct and participate in religious activities. (Article 32)

          ÂêÑÈ°ûÈô¢Ê†°ÂùáÂèØ‰øùÁïôÂÖ∂Ëá™‰∏ªÊÄß‰∏¶‰∫´ÊúâÂ≠∏Ë°ìËá™Áî±ÔºåÂèØÁπºÁ∫åÂæûÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄ‰ª•Â§ñÊãõËÅòÊïôËÅ∑Âì°ÂíåÈÅ∏Áî®ÊïôÊùê„ÄÇ

          ÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÊâÄËæ¶ÁöÑÂ≠∏Ê†°ÂèØÁπºÁ∫åÊèê‰æõÂÆóÊïôÊïôËÇ≤ÔºåÂåÖÊã¨ÈñãË®≠ÂÆóÊïôË™≤Á®ã„ÄÇÂ≠∏Áîü‰∫´ÊúâÈÅ∏ÊìáÈô¢Ê†°ÂíåÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄ‰ª•Â§ñÊ±ÇÂ≠∏ÁöÑËá™Áî±„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨137Ê¢ùÔºâ

          All types of educational institutions may retain their autonomy and enjoy academic freedom. They may continue to recruit teaching staff and select educational materials from outside the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region.

          Schools run by religious organizations may continue to provide religious education, including offering religious courses. Students shall have the freedom to choose their educational institutions and to pursue education outside the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region. (Article 137)

          Chapter VI: ‚ÄúEducation, Science, Culture, Sports, Religion, Labor, and Social Services‚Äù

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÊîøÂ∫ú‰∏çÈôêÂà∂ÂÆóÊïô‰ø°‰ª∞Ëá™Áî±Ôºå‰∏çÂπ≤È†êÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÁöÑÂÖßÈÉ®‰∫ãÂãôÔºå‰∏çÈôêÂà∂ËàáÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÊ≥ïÂæãÊ≤íÊúâÊäµËß∏ÁöÑÂÆóÊïôÊ¥ªÂãï„ÄÇ

          ÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπî‰æùÊ≥ï‰∫´ÊúâË≤°Áî¢ÁöÑÂèñÂæó„ÄÅ‰ΩøÁî®„ÄÅËôïÁΩÆ„ÄÅÁπºÊâø‰ª•ÂèäÊé•ÂèóË≥áÂä©ÁöÑÊ¨äÂà©„ÄÇË≤°Áî¢ÊñπÈù¢ÁöÑÂéüÊúâÊ¨äÁõä‰ªç‰∫à‰øùÊåÅÂíå‰øùË≠∑„ÄÇ

          ÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂèØÊåâÂéüÊúâËæ¶Ê≥ïÁπºÁ∫åËààËæ¶ÂÆóÊïôÈô¢Ê†°„ÄÅÂÖ∂‰ªñÂ≠∏Ê†°„ÄÅÈÜ´Èô¢ÂíåÁ¶èÂà©Ê©üÊßã‰ª•ÂèäÊèê‰æõÂÖ∂‰ªñÁ§æÊúÉÊúçÂãô„ÄÇ

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁöÑÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂíåÊïôÂæíÂèØËàáÂÖ∂‰ªñÂú∞ÊñπÁöÑÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂíåÊïôÂæí‰øùÊåÅÂíåÁôºÂ±ïÈóú‰øÇ„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨141Ê¢ùÔºâ

          The Government of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region shall not restrict the freedom of religious belief, interfere in the internal affairs of religious organizations, or limit religious activities that are not in contravention of the laws of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region.

          Religious organizations shall, in accordance with the law, enjoy the rights to acquire, use, dispose of, and inherit property, as well as to receive donations. Their original property rights and interests shall be maintained and protected.

          Religious organizations may, in accordance with past practices, continue to operate religious schools, other educational institutions, hospitals, and welfare institutions, and provide other social services.

          Religious organizations and believers in the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region may maintain and develop their relations with religious organizations and believers in other places. (Article 141)

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁöÑÊïôËÇ≤„ÄÅÁßëÂ≠∏„ÄÅÊäÄË°ì„ÄÅÊñáÂåñ„ÄÅËóùË°ì„ÄÅÈ´îËÇ≤„ÄÅÂ∞àÊ•≠„ÄÅÈÜ´ÁôÇË°õÁîü„ÄÅÂãûÂ∑•„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÂ∑•‰ΩúÁ≠âÊñπÈù¢ÁöÑÊ∞ëÈñìÂúòÈ´îÂíåÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂêåÂÖßÂú∞Áõ∏ÊáâÁöÑÂúòÈ´îÂíåÁµÑÁπîÁöÑÈóú‰øÇÔºåÊáâ‰ª•‰∫í‰∏çÈö∏Â±¨„ÄÅ‰∫í‰∏çÂπ≤Ê∂âÂíå‰∫íÁõ∏Â∞äÈáçÁöÑÂéüÂâáÁÇ∫Âü∫Á§é„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨148Ê¢ùÔºâ

          The relationship between non-governmental organizations and religious organizations in the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region involved in education, science, technology, culture, arts, sports, professions, healthcare, labor, social welfare, and social work, and their counterparts in the Mainland, shall be based on the principles of non-subordination, non-interference, and mutual respect. (Article 148)

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁöÑÊïôËÇ≤„ÄÅÁßëÂ≠∏„ÄÅÊäÄË°ì„ÄÅÊñáÂåñ„ÄÅËóùË°ì„ÄÅÈ´îËÇ≤„ÄÅÂ∞àÊ•≠„ÄÅÈÜ´ÁôÇË°õÁîü„ÄÅÂãûÂ∑•„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÂ∑•‰ΩúÁ≠âÊñπÈù¢ÁöÑÊ∞ëÈñìÂúòÈ´îÂíåÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂèØÂêå‰∏ñÁïåÂêÑÂúã„ÄÅÂêÑÂú∞ÂçÄÂèäÂúãÈöõÁöÑÊúâÈóúÂúòÈ´îÂíåÁµÑÁπî‰øùÊåÅÂíåÁôºÂ±ïÈóú‰øÇÔºåÂêÑË©≤ÂúòÈ´îÂíåÁµÑÁπîÂèØÊ†πÊìöÈúÄË¶ÅÂÜ†Áî®„Äå‰∏≠ÂúãÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄçÁöÑÂêçÁæ©ÔºåÂèÉËàáÊúâÈóúÊ¥ªÂãï„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨149Ê¢ù Ôºâ

          Non-governmental organizations and religious organizations in the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region involved in education, science, technology, culture, arts, sports, professions, healthcare, labor, social welfare, and social work may maintain and develop relations with relevant organizations and groups in other countries, regions, and international communities. These organizations and groups may, as necessary, use the name ‚ÄúHong Kong, China‚Äù to participate in relevant activities. (Article 149)

          From the finalized provisions of the Basic Law concerning religion, it is evident that the expectations of Hong Kong‚Äôs religious community to maintain the status quo were largely realized. The Chinese concept of the separation of church and state did not appear in the finalized text. The arrangements made by China and the United Kingdom for Hong Kong‚Äôs sovereignty transfer, as noted by Lui Tai-lok, reflect a ‚Äúfreezing Hong Kong‚Äù mindset‚Äîpreserving Hong Kong‚Äôs original model, maintaining the status quo, unchanged for fifty years (Lui 2002: 462). Viewed in this light, the efforts of Hong Kong‚Äôs Christian community before 1997 to safeguard religious freedom and rights, particularly during the discussions and debates in the drafting process of the Basic Law, were motivated by a desire to maintain the status quo. These efforts ultimately resulted in the transplantation of the Basic Law‚Äôs provisions to post-1997 Hong Kong.

          ÂæûÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÊ∂âÂèäÂÆóÊïôÊ¢ùÊñáÁöÑÂÆöÁ®øÂèØË¶ãÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂÆóÊïôÁïåÁà≠ÂèñÁ∂≠ÊåÅÁèæÁãÄÁöÑÊúüÊúõÔºåÂ§ßÊäµÂú®Áõ∏ÈóúÊ¢ùÊ¨æ‰∏≠ËêΩÂØ¶ÔºåËÄå‰∏≠ÂúãÁâàÊú¨ÁöÑÊîøÊïôÂàÜÈõ¢Ê¶ÇÂøµ‰πü‰∏¶Êú™Ë¶ãÊñºÂÆöÁ®ø„ÄÇ‰∏≠Ëã±ÂÖ©ÂúãÂ∞±È¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∏ªÊ¨äÁßª‰∫§ÊâÄ‰ΩúÁöÑÂÆâÊéíÔºåË™†Â¶ÇÂëÇÂ§ßÊ®ÇÊåáÂá∫Ôºå‰πÉÂá∫Êñº‰∏ÄÁ®Æ„ÄåÊÄ•ÂáçÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄçÁöÑÊÄùÁ∂≠ÔºåÂç≥ÊääÂéüÊúâÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁöÑÊ®°Âºè„ÄåÂáùÂõ∫Ëµ∑‰æÜ„ÄçÔºåÁ∂≠ÊåÅÁèæÁãÄÔºå‰∫îÂçÅÂπ¥‰∏çËÆäÔºàÂëÇÂ§ßÊ®Ç2002Ôºö462Ôºâ„ÄÇÈÄôÊ®£Áúã‰æÜÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂü∫Áù£Êïô‰∫∫Â£´Âú®‰πù‰∏ÉÂâçÂ∞çÂÆóÊïôËá™Áî±ÂèäÊ¨äÁõäÁöÑÁà≠ÂèñÔºåÁâπÂà•Âú®Âü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïËµ∑ËçâÈÅéÁ®ãÁöÑË®éË´ñÂèäËæØË´ñÔºå‰πüÊòØÂá∫ÊñºÈÄôÁ®ÆÁ∂≠ÊåÅÁèæÁãÄÁöÑËÄÉÊÖÆÔºåÊúÄÂæåÈÄöÈÅéÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÂæó‰ª•ÁßªÊ§çËá≥‰πù‰∏ÉÂæåÁöÑÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ

          1. Hong Kong Christianity Under the Post-Transition Chinese Framework (Hing Fuk-tsang 2018: 208, 214)

            In 1985, the composition of the Basic Law Drafting and Consultative Committees followed the Chinese government‚Äôs model of representatives from various sectors, as seen in the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference. During the post-transition period, religious representatives were allocated seats within Chinese frameworks, forming a ‚Äúreligious representative‚Äù mechanism:

            Due to the Sino-British dispute over Governor Chris Patten‚Äôs political reforms, the Chinese government established the ‚ÄúHong Kong Affairs Advisors‚Äù in 1992 to ensure a smooth transition and maintain prosperity and stability. The term lasted until June 30, 1997. A total of 187 advisors were appointed in four batches between March 1992, including Anglican Bishop Peter Kwong of the Diocese of Hong Kong and Macau, who was appointed as a Christian Affairs Advisor.

            On January 26, 1996, the Preparatory Committee for the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region was established based on the decision made at the Third Session of the Seventh National People‚Äôs Congress on April 4, 1990. The committee comprised 150 members, 94 from Hong Kong and 56 from Mainland China. Bishop Peter Kwong was appointed as a member of the Preparatory Committee.

            In 1996, the Election Committee for the First Government of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region was formed by the Preparatory Committee. It was responsible for selecting the first Chief Executive and the Provisional Legislative Council. The Christian representatives elected to the committee were Bishop Peter Kwong, Rev. Philip Tse (Chairman of the Hong Kong Christian Council), and Ms. Yuk Tak-fun (General Secretary of the Hong Kong YWCA).

            After 1997, the Beijing government strengthened its engagement with religious figures. The most notable measure was appointing religious leaders as delegates to the National Committee of the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference (CPPCC) as ‚ÄúSpecially Invited Hong Kong Members.‚Äù For Christianity, Bishop Peter Kwong was appointed to the 9th and 10th CPPCC in 1998 and 2003, respectively. After Bishop Paul Kwong succeeded him as the Archbishop and Primate of the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui in 2007, he was appointed to the 12th and 13th CPPCC in 2013 and 2018. In 2020, Archbishop Andrew Chan, who succeeded Bishop Paul Kwong, was appointed to the 14th CPPCC in 2023.

            ‚Äî

            1. Government‚Äôs First Funding for Gospel-Based Drug Rehabilitation, 1998 (Lee 2001: 73‚Äì79; Christian Times 1998)

              Historically, the government did not recognize the effectiveness of gospel-based drug rehabilitation programs. However, following the second drug summit in May 1996, chaired by Governor Chris Patten, the government decided to include non-governmental organizations (NGOs) in its funding scope, contingent on an evaluation of their rehabilitation programs. In January 1997, the Social Welfare Department commissioned Professor Patrick Chan Kai-nai of the Chinese University of Hong Kong‚Äôs Faculty of Medicine to conduct a relevant study.

              By December 1997, five organizations were confirmed to meet the Social Welfare Department‚Äôs criteria and were deemed eligible for funding: Barnabas Charitable Services Association, Christian New Being Fellowship, Ling Oi Youth Centre (Finnish Evangelical Lutheran Mission), Operation Dawn‚Äôs Island Centre, and St. Stephen‚Äôs Society.

              In March 1998, the Social Welfare Department began funding four NGOs providing voluntary residential drug rehabilitation programs: Barnabas Charitable Services Association, Ling Oi Youth Centre, Operation Dawn, and Christian New Being Fellowship (St. Stephen‚Äôs Society declined funding as it had secured alternative financial support). These programs varied in length and content but were all faith-based, emphasizing psychosocial recovery counseling and offering follow-up support services. For the 1998‚Äì99 fiscal year, the government allocated over HK$16 million to these programs.

              1. Buddha‚Äôs Birthday Recognized as a Public Holiday for the First Time, 1999 (Buddhist Door, 2024)

                On May 22, 1999, Buddha‚Äôs Birthday was officially celebrated as a public holiday in Hong Kong for the first time. The Hong Kong Buddhist Association made a special request to the Chinese government and the Buddhist Association of China to bring the Buddha‚Äôs tooth relic, a national treasure from Lingguang Temple in Beijing, to Hong Kong. On that day, the Hong Kong Buddhist community held a grand celebration and veneration ceremony at the Hong Kong Coliseum, officiated by Elder Sik Kok-kwong. Prominent attendees included Zhao Puchu, Vice Chairman of the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference and President of the Buddhist Association of China, and Tung Chee-hwa, Chief Executive of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region (HKSAR).

                As early as 1961, Sik Kok-kwong of the Hong Kong Buddhist Association had petitioned the Hong Kong government to recognize Buddha‚Äôs Birthday as a public holiday. The Director of Home Affairs expressed willingness to assist. The association launched a ‚Äú100,000 Signatures Campaign‚Äù to appeal to the government. Two years later, the association collected 120,000 signatures, which Sik Kok-kwong submitted to the Director of Home Affairs for forwarding to the Governor. While the government promised to consider the request, no decision was made for years.

                In 1985, Sik Kok-kwong, appointed as a member of the Basic Law Drafting Committee, raised the issue again during the committee‚Äôs discussions.

                In 1998, he renewed his appeal to the HKSAR government, submitting an official request, Application for Implementing Buddha‚Äôs Birthday as a Public Holiday in 1999, and a related petition to the Xinhua News Agency Hong Kong Branch. The petition emphasized that ‚ÄúHong Kong, under British rule for many years, had favored Western religious holidays, while holidays for religions predominantly followed by Chinese residents were largely absent, leading to a sense of discrimination. For equality, Buddha‚Äôs Birthday should be designated as a public holiday.‚Äù

                In 1998, the HKSAR government submitted the Holidays (Amendment) Ordinance 1998. A government spokesperson stated: ‚ÄúThe primary purpose of the ordinance is to designate Labor Day (May 1) and Buddha‚Äôs Birthday (the eighth day of the fourth lunar month) as public holidays starting in 1999, replacing the two public holidays observed in 1997 and 1998‚ÄîVictory Day of the Anti-Japanese War (the third Monday of August) and the day following National Day (October 2).‚Äù

                1. Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Ordinance, Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation Ordinance, and Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee Ordinance, 1999 (Press Release, 1999)

                  Following the establishment ceremony of the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui (Hong Kong Anglican Church Province) on October 25, 1998, the Secretary for Home Affairs signed a notice at the request of the church to enact the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Ordinance (Cap. 1157), the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee Ordinance (Cap. 1158), and the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation Ordinance (Cap. 1159). These ordinances came into effect on February 26, 1999, and were gazetted the same day.

                  The Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Ordinance established the ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Province‚Äù as a corporate body under the name ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui‚Äù and provided provisions regarding its composition, powers, responsibilities, and related matters.

                  The Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation Ordinance provided for the continuation of the corporation formerly known as the ‚ÄúBishop of Victoria‚Äù under the name ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation.‚Äù It outlined the foundation‚Äôs composition, powers, and responsibilities while repealing the Bishop of Victoria Incorporation Ordinance.

                  The Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee Ordinance ensured the continuation of the corporation formerly known as the ‚ÄúDiocesan Trusts Committee‚Äù under the name ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee.‚Äù It specified the committee‚Äôs composition, powers, and responsibilities, while repealing the Diocesan Trusts Committee Incorporation Ordinance.

                  1. The ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù Debate, 1995‚Äì1996 (Institute of Sino-Christian Studies, 1997; Chan Shing-king, 1998; Lee 2010: 53‚Äì62)

                    From September 1995 to May 1996, a ten-month-long debate on ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù unfolded in the weekly publication Christian Times. Eight individuals participated in the discussion: four from Hong Kong, three from Mainland China, and one from Taiwan. The Hong Kong contributors represented both evangelical and ecumenical traditions, while the Mainland contributors included academics and Christian researchers. The debate centered on how to understand the phenomenon of ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù in China and how Hong Kong‚Äôs theological community evaluated it. Central to the discussion were issues of orthodox theological authority and the representation of theology, particularly in relation to debates with Mainland scholars.

                    The debate was initiated by Lo Ping-cheung of the Department of Religion and Philosophy at Hong Kong Baptist University, who published the article ‚ÄúChina‚Äôs Apollos and the 1997 Crisis for Hong Kong Theology.‚Äù Lo likened the interest in Christian culture among Mainland Chinese intellectuals and scholars to ‚Äúthe Macedonian Call.‚Äù He noted that those responding to this call were not the Mainland churches or seminaries but scholars engaged in Christian studies at universities, whom he referred to as ‚ÄúChina‚Äôs Apollos.‚Äù He specifically highlighted figures such as Liu Xiaofeng (working at Tao Fong Shan Christian Centre and the Institute of Chinese Studies at The Chinese University of Hong Kong), He Guanghu, Zhuo Xinping, and Tang Yi (from the Chinese Academy of Social Sciences), as well as Zhao Dunhua (Peking University). Lo urged Hong Kong‚Äôs theological community to broaden its perspective beyond Hong Kong and focus on China as a whole. Failure to do so, he argued, would risk losing theological influence not only in China but also in Hong Kong itself, relegating Hong Kong theology to a passive role (Lo 1995).

                    In response, Leung Ka-lun of the Alliance Bible Seminary wrote ‚ÄúIs This Another Debt We Owe?‚Äù He argued that the traditional church and theological community must acknowledge that they no longer hold exclusive representational authority over Christianity or the right to limit others‚Äô voices. While accepting that losing some authority was inevitable, Leung warned against over-praising the so-called ‚ÄúChina‚Äôs Apollos‚Äù and emphasized the need to critically assess whether their non-orthodox approaches could lead to misunderstandings of Christianity. At the same time, Leung stressed that Hong Kong‚Äôs churches and theological community should not be burdened with the overwhelming responsibility of shaping the future of both Hong Kong and Chinese culture (Leung 1995a).

                    Kwok Tak-wai of the Divinity School of Chung Chi College responded by asserting that Hong Kong‚Äôs theological community neither could nor should aim to be the theological spokesperson for China. He also argued that the influence of Western theologians, who were often unfamiliar with the local context but still ‚Äúpreached‚Äù in Hong Kong, posed a greater threat than ‚ÄúChina‚Äôs Apollos‚Äù (Kwok 1995).

                    Lee King-heung, former president of the Christian Study Centre on Chinese Religion and Culture, elaborated on the concept of ‚Äútheological authority.‚Äù He argued that true theological authority derives from understanding God‚Äôs teachings and questioned whether ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù could fully represent orthodox theology, given their focus on translating and introducing foreign theological ideas (Lee 1995).

                    Responses from Mainland contributors included Li Qiuling (Renmin University of China), Zhang Xianyong (Nanjing Union Theological Seminary), and Wu Bofan (Chinese Academy of Social Sciences), writing under the pseudonym ‚ÄúBo Fan.‚Äù Li criticized the self-proclaimed orthodoxy of some in Hong Kong‚Äôs theological community, asserting that no denomination or individual could claim an exclusive understanding of Christian faith (Li 1995). Similarly, Bo Fan rejected the principle of ‚Äúorthodoxy‚Äù proposed by Hong Kong theologians but noted that ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù approach theology as a personal ‚Äúconfession of faith,‚Äù with cultural rather than ecclesiastical dimensions (Bo Fan 1995). Zhang, with a background in church and theological education in China, distinguished between ‚Äúinsiders‚Äù and ‚Äúoutsiders,‚Äù asserting that ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù had no intention of competing for theological authority in Hong Kong (Zhang 1996).

                    Leung Ka-lun responded to Zhang, emphasizing that his concern was not about drawing boundaries around the church but about how ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù understood Christianity. He argued that Christianity is a historical faith and warned against reductionist approaches that risk fragmenting the understanding of Christianity (Leung 1996).

                    In Taiwan, Chen Ching-pao analyzed the debate through the lenses of ‚Äúecclesiastical theology‚Äù and ‚Äúhumanistic theology.‚Äù He argued that ecclesiastical theology, rooted in pastoral care and evangelism, lacked the capacity to address modern phenomena. Since ‚ÄúCultural Christians‚Äù were not part of the pastoral system or converted through evangelism, their discourse primarily drew from cultural and intellectual contexts (Chen 1996).

                    In 1997, the Institute of Sino-Christian Studies compiled the debate and subsequent discussions into a publication titled Cultural Christians: Phenomena and Controversies.

                    1. Christian Evangelism Provokes Buddhist Dissatisfaction, 1999 (Christian Times, 1999)

                      On May 22, 1999, the first public holiday for Buddha‚Äôs Birthday in Hong Kong, the Asia for Christ Mission Association held an event titled ‚ÄúThe First Priority: Evangelism Techniques Training Day.‚Äù The promotional materials claimed, ‚ÄúAt a time when many are seeking religious solace, Satan seizes the opportunity to steal souls.‚Äù They also noted that Buddha‚Äôs Birthday fell on the eve of Pentecost, framing it as a ‚Äúspiritual battle.‚Äù The event included activities such as ‚Äúrepentance for idol worship among Chinese people‚Äù and training on ‚Äúhow to evangelize to Buddhists.‚Äù

                      This publicity sparked dissatisfaction among some Buddhists, who initially planned to issue a public response. However, with mediation by a third party, the matter was resolved amicably and handled with minimal publicity.

                        1. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´„ÄåÊñáÁçª „Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†Áõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

                      1. Sino-British Relations Post-Tiananmen Incident (Carroll 2007: 191‚Äì195, 198‚Äì203; Lau Chi-pang & Lau Shu-wing, 2019: 436‚Äì439, 443‚Äì447)

                        After the Tiananmen Incident in 1989, the United Kingdom altered its policy toward China, leading to a deterioration in Sino-British relations. Western nations adopted a containment strategy against the Chinese Communist Party (CCP), isolating China diplomatically. Meanwhile, the collapse of Communist regimes in the Soviet Union and Eastern Europe fueled Western beliefs in ‚Äúthe end of history,‚Äù where liberal democracy would triumph over Communism.

                        In April 1994, the UK Parliament‚Äôs Foreign Affairs Committee released a report explicitly stating that the British government did not believe the Beijing regime would necessarily remain in power until 1997. Following Tiananmen, the UK unilaterally suspended a planned meeting of the Sino-British Joint Liaison Group in July. In 1991, the UK enacted the Bill of Rights Ordinance, which nullified existing Hong Kong laws that conflicted with the ordinance and required future laws to comply with it. This drew strong criticism from Beijing.

                        Sino-British tensions also affected major infrastructure projects, such as the new Hong Kong airport. In October 1989, Governor David Wilson announced the ‚ÄúRose Garden Project,‚Äù proposing a new airport at Chek Lap Kok on Lantau Island. The existing Kai Tak Airport was overburdened, and the project aimed to lift Hong Kong‚Äôs post-Tiananmen malaise. However, the announcement was made without prior consultation with Beijing, raising concerns in China that the plan would deplete Hong Kong‚Äôs reserves, leaving the future HKSAR government burdened with debt. Negotiations ensued, as the UK recognized it needed China‚Äôs support to secure funding for the project beyond 1997. In September 1991, UK Prime Minister John Major visited Beijing, breaking the diplomatic freeze since Tiananmen. Both sides signed a memorandum of understanding: the UK committed to leaving HK$250 billion in reserves for the HKSAR government, while China agreed to support the airport project.

                        In April 1992, John Major appointed Christopher Patten as the last Governor of Hong Kong. In October, Patten proposed political reforms in his Policy Address, increasing directly elected seats in the Legislative Council from 18 to 20, expanding the voter base for functional constituencies, and lowering the voting age from 21 to 18. These changes significantly boosted democracy in the 1995 Legislative Council elections. Beijing, however, strongly opposed the reforms, calling them violations of the Sino-British Joint Declaration, the Basic Law, and mutual agreements. Seventeen rounds of talks between April and November 1993 failed to resolve the disputes.

                        Beijing subsequently decided that members of Hong Kong‚Äôs last Legislative Council, municipal councils, and district boards would not ‚Äútransition‚Äù past June 30, 1997. Instead, a Provisional Legislative Council was formed in Shenzhen in 1996.

                        ‚Äî

                        1. Hong Kong-China Relations from Tiananmen to 1997 (Carroll 2007: 197‚Äì198, 215; Chong Yiu-kwong & Tsui Ka-wing, 2014: 101‚Äì106)

                          In the early 1990s, Hong Kong could not escape the shadow of the Tiananmen Massacre. Public confidence plummeted, with widespread anxiety about the post-1997 future. Despite this, the first Legislative Council elections with direct voting were held in 1991. Pro-democracy candidates won 17 of the 18 directly elected seats and several functional constituencies, reflecting the ‚ÄúTiananmen effect.‚Äù

                          Meanwhile, the drafting of the Basic Law, which began in the mid-1980s, was completed in February 1990 and approved in April that year by the National People‚Äôs Congress (NPC). The CCP, concerned about Hong Kong becoming a ‚Äúbase of anti-Communist subversion,‚Äù added Article 23, mandating HKSAR legislation to prohibit acts such as treason, secession, sedition, subversion, and foreign political activities. Provisions were also added to limit Legislative Council powers.

                          On June 30, 1997, the Sino-British handover ceremony took place, and the HKSAR government was established on July 1. Democratic legislators, prematurely dismissed, vowed on the Legislative Council balcony to ‚Äúreturn.‚Äù The Provisional Legislative Council operated from January 1997 to April 1998, passing significant legislation, including amendments to the Public Order Ordinance and abolishing collective bargaining rights. Critics argue that post-1997 Hong Kong underwent ‚Äúrecolonization,‚Äù with Beijing replacing London as the sovereign power, raising concerns about whether ‚Äúone country, two systems‚Äù could sustain Hong Kong‚Äôs unique way of life.

                          ‚Äî

                          1. Deng Xiaoping‚Äôs Southern Tour and Shifts in U.S. Policy Toward China (Xu Cheng-en, 2023: 279‚Äì285; Carroll 2007: 210‚Äì211)

                            In 1992, Deng Xiaoping‚Äôs southern tour reaffirmed the policy of reform and opening up, advancing China‚Äôs economic transformation and improving Sino-U.S. relations.

                            Later that year, Bill Clinton was elected U.S. president, promoting the ‚ÄúDemocratic Enlargement‚Äù policy to replace Cold War dynamics with global integration. Under this framework, the U.S. and other Western nations shifted from containment to engagement with China, hoping economic reforms would lead to political liberalization. Congress leaned toward granting China most-favored-nation status and delinking trade policies from human rights issues. With U.S. support, China further integrated into global trade, culminating in its 2001 entry into the World Trade Organization (WTO).

                            The lifting of trade sanctions by Western countries spurred foreign investment, much of which flowed through Hong Kong into China, benefiting Hong Kong‚Äôs economy. This deepened economic interdependence between Hong Kong and the Mainland.

                            The 1992 U.S.‚ÄìHong Kong Policy Act allowed the U.S. to treat Hong Kong as a separate territory from China post-1997, provided it maintained ‚Äúsufficient autonomy.‚Äù

                            ‚Äî

                            1. Asian Financial Crisis After 1997 (Lui Tai-lok, 2002)

                              While political issues dominated pre-1997 concerns, post-1997 Hong Kong faced severe economic challenges, including the Asian Financial Crisis in late 1997. The crisis burst bubbles in Hong Kong‚Äôs stock and real estate markets, causing plummeting prices and widespread ‚Äúnegative equity.‚Äù Rising unemployment exacerbated social and economic problems.

                              Sociologist Lui Tai-lok observed that Hong Kong society, starting on July 1, 1997, experienced systemic failure. Institutional arrangements devised in the 1980s to preserve stability effectively collapsed in the face of the crisis.

        ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

        1. Promulgation of the Basic Law and Religious Provisions, 1990 (Basic Law 1990)

          In April 1990, the Third Session of the Seventh National People‚Äôs Congress promulgated the Basic Law, which included the following provisions related to religion:

          (1)Âü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÈ†í‰ΩàËàáÂÆóÊïôÊ¢ùÊ¨æ 1990ÔºàÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ï1990Ôºâ

          1990Âπ¥4ÊúàÔºå‰∏ÉÂ±ÜÂÖ®Âúã‰∫∫Â§ß‰∏âÊ¨°ÊúÉË≠∞È†í‰ΩàÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÔºåÂÖ∂‰∏≠Ê∂âÂèäÂÆóÊïôÁöÑÊ¢ùÊ¨æÂ¶Ç‰∏ãÔºö

          Á¨¨‰∏âÁ´†„ÄåÂ±ÖÊ∞ëÁöÑÂü∫Êú¨Ê¨äÂà©ÂíåÁæ©Âãô„Äç

          Chapter III: ‚ÄúFundamental Rights and Duties of Residents‚Äù

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÂ±ÖÊ∞ëÊúâ‰ø°‰ª∞ÁöÑËá™Áî±„ÄÇÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂ±ÖÊ∞ëÊúâÂÆóÊïô‰ø°‰ª∞ÁöÑËá™Áî±ÔºåÊúâÂÖ¨ÈñãÂÇ≥ÊïôÂíåËàâË°å„ÄÅÂèÉÂä†ÂÆóÊïôÊ¥ªÂãïÁöÑËá™Áî±„ÄÇÔºàÁ¨¨32Ê¢ùÔºâ

          Hong Kong residents shall have freedom of belief. Hong Kong residents shall enjoy the freedom of religious belief, the freedom to preach publicly, and the freedom to conduct and participate in religious activities. (Article 32)

          ÂêÑÈ°ûÈô¢Ê†°ÂùáÂèØ‰øùÁïôÂÖ∂Ëá™‰∏ªÊÄß‰∏¶‰∫´ÊúâÂ≠∏Ë°ìËá™Áî±ÔºåÂèØÁπºÁ∫åÂæûÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄ‰ª•Â§ñÊãõËÅòÊïôËÅ∑Âì°ÂíåÈÅ∏Áî®ÊïôÊùê„ÄÇ

          ÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÊâÄËæ¶ÁöÑÂ≠∏Ê†°ÂèØÁπºÁ∫åÊèê‰æõÂÆóÊïôÊïôËÇ≤ÔºåÂåÖÊã¨ÈñãË®≠ÂÆóÊïôË™≤Á®ã„ÄÇÂ≠∏Áîü‰∫´ÊúâÈÅ∏ÊìáÈô¢Ê†°ÂíåÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄ‰ª•Â§ñÊ±ÇÂ≠∏ÁöÑËá™Áî±„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨137Ê¢ùÔºâ

          All types of educational institutions may retain their autonomy and enjoy academic freedom. They may continue to recruit teaching staff and select educational materials from outside the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region.

          Schools run by religious organizations may continue to provide religious education, including offering religious courses. Students shall have the freedom to choose their educational institutions and to pursue education outside the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region. (Article 137)

          Á¨¨ÂÖ≠Á´†„ÄåÊïôËÇ≤„ÄÅÁßëÂ≠∏„ÄÅÊñáÂåñ„ÄÅÈ´îËÇ≤„ÄÅÂÆóÊïô„ÄÅÂãûÂ∑•ÂíåÁ§æÊúÉÊúçÂãô„Äç

          Chapter VI: ‚ÄúEducation, Science, Culture, Sports, Religion, Labor, and Social Services‚Äù

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÊîøÂ∫ú‰∏çÈôêÂà∂ÂÆóÊïô‰ø°‰ª∞Ëá™Áî±Ôºå‰∏çÂπ≤È†êÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÁöÑÂÖßÈÉ®‰∫ãÂãôÔºå‰∏çÈôêÂà∂ËàáÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÊ≥ïÂæãÊ≤íÊúâÊäµËß∏ÁöÑÂÆóÊïôÊ¥ªÂãï„ÄÇ

          ÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπî‰æùÊ≥ï‰∫´ÊúâË≤°Áî¢ÁöÑÂèñÂæó„ÄÅ‰ΩøÁî®„ÄÅËôïÁΩÆ„ÄÅÁπºÊâø‰ª•ÂèäÊé•ÂèóË≥áÂä©ÁöÑÊ¨äÂà©„ÄÇË≤°Áî¢ÊñπÈù¢ÁöÑÂéüÊúâÊ¨äÁõä‰ªç‰∫à‰øùÊåÅÂíå‰øùË≠∑„ÄÇ

          ÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂèØÊåâÂéüÊúâËæ¶Ê≥ïÁπºÁ∫åËààËæ¶ÂÆóÊïôÈô¢Ê†°„ÄÅÂÖ∂‰ªñÂ≠∏Ê†°„ÄÅÈÜ´Èô¢ÂíåÁ¶èÂà©Ê©üÊßã‰ª•ÂèäÊèê‰æõÂÖ∂‰ªñÁ§æÊúÉÊúçÂãô„ÄÇ

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁöÑÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂíåÊïôÂæíÂèØËàáÂÖ∂‰ªñÂú∞ÊñπÁöÑÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂíåÊïôÂæí‰øùÊåÅÂíåÁôºÂ±ïÈóú‰øÇ„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨141Ê¢ùÔºâ

          The Government of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region shall not restrict the freedom of religious belief, interfere in the internal affairs of religious organizations, or limit religious activities that are not in contravention of the laws of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region.

          Religious organizations shall, in accordance with the law, enjoy the rights to acquire, use, dispose of, and inherit property, as well as to receive donations. Their original property rights and interests shall be maintained and protected.

          Religious organizations may, in accordance with past practices, continue to operate religious schools, other educational institutions, hospitals, and welfare institutions, and provide other social services.

          Religious organizations and believers in the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region may maintain and develop their relations with religious organizations and believers in other places. (Article 141)

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁöÑÊïôËÇ≤„ÄÅÁßëÂ≠∏„ÄÅÊäÄË°ì„ÄÅÊñáÂåñ„ÄÅËóùË°ì„ÄÅÈ´îËÇ≤„ÄÅÂ∞àÊ•≠„ÄÅÈÜ´ÁôÇË°õÁîü„ÄÅÂãûÂ∑•„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÂ∑•‰ΩúÁ≠âÊñπÈù¢ÁöÑÊ∞ëÈñìÂúòÈ´îÂíåÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂêåÂÖßÂú∞Áõ∏ÊáâÁöÑÂúòÈ´îÂíåÁµÑÁπîÁöÑÈóú‰øÇÔºåÊáâ‰ª•‰∫í‰∏çÈö∏Â±¨„ÄÅ‰∫í‰∏çÂπ≤Ê∂âÂíå‰∫íÁõ∏Â∞äÈáçÁöÑÂéüÂâáÁÇ∫Âü∫Á§é„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨148Ê¢ùÔºâ

          The relationship between non-governmental organizations and religious organizations in the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region involved in education, science, technology, culture, arts, sports, professions, healthcare, labor, social welfare, and social work, and their counterparts in the Mainland, shall be based on the principles of non-subordination, non-interference, and mutual respect. (Article 148)

          È¶ôÊ∏ØÁâπÂà•Ë°åÊîøÂçÄÁöÑÊïôËÇ≤„ÄÅÁßëÂ≠∏„ÄÅÊäÄË°ì„ÄÅÊñáÂåñ„ÄÅËóùË°ì„ÄÅÈ´îËÇ≤„ÄÅÂ∞àÊ•≠„ÄÅÈÜ´ÁôÇË°õÁîü„ÄÅÂãûÂ∑•„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©„ÄÅÁ§æÊúÉÂ∑•‰ΩúÁ≠âÊñπÈù¢ÁöÑÊ∞ëÈñìÂúòÈ´îÂíåÂÆóÊïôÁµÑÁπîÂèØÂêå‰∏ñÁïåÂêÑÂúã„ÄÅÂêÑÂú∞ÂçÄÂèäÂúãÈöõÁöÑÊúâÈóúÂúòÈ´îÂíåÁµÑÁπî‰øùÊåÅÂíåÁôºÂ±ïÈóú‰øÇÔºåÂêÑË©≤ÂúòÈ´îÂíåÁµÑÁπîÂèØÊ†πÊìöÈúÄË¶ÅÂÜ†Áî®„Äå‰∏≠ÂúãÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄçÁöÑÂêçÁæ©ÔºåÂèÉËàáÊúâÈóúÊ¥ªÂãï„ÄÇ ÔºàÁ¨¨149Ê¢ù Ôºâ

          Non-governmental organizations and religious organizations in the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region involved in education, science, technology, culture, arts, sports, professions, healthcare, labor, social welfare, and social work may maintain and develop relations with relevant organizations and groups in other countries, regions, and international communities. These organizations and groups may, as necessary, use the name ‚ÄúHong Kong, China‚Äù to participate in relevant activities. (Article 149)

          From the finalized provisions of the Basic Law concerning religion, it is evident that the expectations of Hong Kong‚Äôs religious community to maintain the status quo were largely realized. The Chinese concept of the separation of church and state did not appear in the finalized text. The arrangements made by China and the United Kingdom for Hong Kong‚Äôs sovereignty transfer, as noted by Lui Tai-lok, reflect a ‚Äúfreezing Hong Kong‚Äù mindset‚Äîpreserving Hong Kong‚Äôs original model, maintaining the status quo, unchanged for fifty years (Lui 2002: 462). Viewed in this light, the efforts of Hong Kong‚Äôs Christian community before 1997 to safeguard religious freedom and rights, particularly during the discussions and debates in the drafting process of the Basic Law, were motivated by a desire to maintain the status quo. These efforts ultimately resulted in the transplantation of the Basic Law‚Äôs provisions to post-1997 Hong Kong.

          ÂæûÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÊ∂âÂèäÂÆóÊïôÊ¢ùÊñáÁöÑÂÆöÁ®øÂèØË¶ãÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂÆóÊïôÁïåÁà≠ÂèñÁ∂≠ÊåÅÁèæÁãÄÁöÑÊúüÊúõÔºåÂ§ßÊäµÂú®Áõ∏ÈóúÊ¢ùÊ¨æ‰∏≠ËêΩÂØ¶ÔºåËÄå‰∏≠ÂúãÁâàÊú¨ÁöÑÊîøÊïôÂàÜÈõ¢Ê¶ÇÂøµ‰πü‰∏¶Êú™Ë¶ãÊñºÂÆöÁ®ø„ÄÇ‰∏≠Ëã±ÂÖ©ÂúãÂ∞±È¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∏ªÊ¨äÁßª‰∫§ÊâÄ‰ΩúÁöÑÂÆâÊéíÔºåË™†Â¶ÇÂëÇÂ§ßÊ®ÇÊåáÂá∫Ôºå‰πÉÂá∫Êñº‰∏ÄÁ®Æ„ÄåÊÄ•ÂáçÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄçÁöÑÊÄùÁ∂≠ÔºåÂç≥ÊääÂéüÊúâÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁöÑÊ®°Âºè„ÄåÂáùÂõ∫Ëµ∑‰æÜ„ÄçÔºåÁ∂≠ÊåÅÁèæÁãÄÔºå‰∫îÂçÅÂπ¥‰∏çËÆäÔºàÂëÇÂ§ßÊ®Ç2002Ôºö462Ôºâ„ÄÇÈÄôÊ®£Áúã‰æÜÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂü∫Áù£Êïô‰∫∫Â£´Âú®‰πù‰∏ÉÂâçÂ∞çÂÆóÊïôËá™Áî±ÂèäÊ¨äÁõäÁöÑÁà≠ÂèñÔºåÁâπÂà•Âú®Âü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïËµ∑ËçâÈÅéÁ®ãÁöÑË®éË´ñÂèäËæØË´ñÔºå‰πüÊòØÂá∫ÊñºÈÄôÁ®ÆÁ∂≠ÊåÅÁèæÁãÄÁöÑËÄÉÊÖÆÔºåÊúÄÂæåÈÄöÈÅéÂü∫Êú¨Ê≥ïÂæó‰ª•ÁßªÊ§çËá≥‰πù‰∏ÉÂæåÁöÑÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ

          1. Hong Kong Christianity Under the Post-Transition Chinese Framework (Hing Fuk-tsang 2018: 208, 214)

            In 1985, the composition of the Basic Law Drafting and Consultative Committees followed the Chinese government‚Äôs model of representatives from various sectors, as seen in the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference. During the post-transition period, religious representatives were allocated seats within Chinese frameworks, forming a ‚Äúreligious representative‚Äù mechanism:

            Due to the Sino-British dispute over Governor Chris Patten‚Äôs political reforms, the Chinese government established the ‚ÄúHong Kong Affairs Advisors‚Äù in 1992 to ensure a smooth transition and maintain prosperity and stability. The term lasted until June 30, 1997. A total of 187 advisors were appointed in four batches between March 1992, including Anglican Bishop Peter Kwong of the Diocese of Hong Kong and Macau, who was appointed as a Christian Affairs Advisor.

            On January 26, 1996, the Preparatory Committee for the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region was established based on the decision made at the Third Session of the Seventh National People‚Äôs Congress on April 4, 1990. The committee comprised 150 members, 94 from Hong Kong and 56 from Mainland China. Bishop Peter Kwong was appointed as a member of the Preparatory Committee.

            In 1996, the Election Committee for the First Government of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region was formed by the Preparatory Committee. It was responsible for selecting the first Chief Executive and the Provisional Legislative Council. The Christian representatives elected to the committee were Bishop Peter Kwong, Rev. Philip Tse (Chairman of the Hong Kong Christian Council), and Ms. Yuk Tak-fun (General Secretary of the Hong Kong YWCA).

            After 1997, the Beijing government strengthened its engagement with religious figures. The most notable measure was appointing religious leaders as delegates to the National Committee of the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference (CPPCC) as ‚ÄúSpecially Invited Hong Kong Members.‚Äù For Christianity, Bishop Peter Kwong was appointed to the 9th and 10th CPPCC in 1998 and 2003, respectively. After Bishop Paul Kwong succeeded him as the Archbishop and Primate of the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui in 2007, he was appointed to the 12th and 13th CPPCC in 2013 and 2018. In 2020, Archbishop Andrew Chan, who succeeded Bishop Paul Kwong, was appointed to the 14th CPPCC in 2023.

            ‚Äî

            1. Government‚Äôs First Funding for Gospel-Based Drug Rehabilitation, 1998 (Lee 2001: 73‚Äì79; Christian Times 1998)

              Historically, the government did not recognize the effectiveness of gospel-based drug rehabilitation programs. However, following the second drug summit in May 1996, chaired by Governor Chris Patten, the government decided to include non-governmental organizations (NGOs) in its funding scope, contingent on an evaluation of their rehabilitation programs. In January 1997, the Social Welfare Department commissioned Professor Patrick Chan Kai-nai of the Chinese University of Hong Kong‚Äôs Faculty of Medicine to conduct a relevant study.

              By December 1997, five organizations were confirmed to meet the Social Welfare Department‚Äôs criteria and were deemed eligible for funding: Barnabas Charitable Services Association, Christian New Being Fellowship, Ling Oi Youth Centre (Finnish Evangelical Lutheran Mission), Operation Dawn‚Äôs Island Centre, and St. Stephen‚Äôs Society.

              In March 1998, the Social Welfare Department began funding four NGOs providing voluntary residential drug rehabilitation programs: Barnabas Charitable Services Association, Ling Oi Youth Centre, Operation Dawn, and Christian New Being Fellowship (St. Stephen‚Äôs Society declined funding as it had secured alternative financial support). These programs varied in length and content but were all faith-based, emphasizing psychosocial recovery counseling and offering follow-up support services. For the 1998‚Äì99 fiscal year, the government allocated over HK$16 million to these programs.

              ‰∏ÄÁõ¥‰ª•‰æÜÔºåÊîøÂ∫úÂ∞çÁ¶èÈü≥ÊàíÊØíÁöÑÊàêÊïà‰∏ç‰∫àÊâøË™ç„ÄÇÁõ¥Ëá≥1996Âπ¥5ÊúàÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØËàâË°åÁ¨¨‰∫åÊ¨°ÊØíÂìÅÂïèÈ°åÈ´òÂ≥∞ÊúÉË≠∞ÔºåÁî±Ê∏ØÁù£ÂΩ≠ÂÆöÂ∫∑ÔºàChristopher PattenÔºâ‰∏ªÊåÅ„ÄÇÊúÉÂæåÔºåÊîøÂ∫úÊ±∫ÂÆöÊääÈùûÊîøÂ∫úÊ©üÊßãÁ¥çÂÖ•Ë≥áÂä©ÁØÑÂúçÔºå‰ΩÜÈúÄË¶ÅÂÖàÂ∞±ÈÄô‰∫õÊ©üÊßãÊèê‰æõÁöÑÁôÇÂ∫∑Ë®àÂäÉÊàêÊïàÈÄ≤Ë°åË©ï‰º∞„ÄÇÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©ÁΩ≤Êñº1997Âπ¥1ÊúàÂßîË®óÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∏≠ÊñáÂ§ßÂ≠∏ÈÜ´Â≠∏Èô¢Èô≥‰Ω≥ÈºêÊïôÊéàÈÄ≤Ë°åÁõ∏ÈóúÁ†îÁ©∂„ÄÇ1997Âπ¥12ÊúàÔºåÊúâ5ÈñìÊ©üÊßãË¢´Ë≠âÂØ¶Á¨¶ÂêàÁ§æÁΩ≤ÊâÄË®ÇÊ∫ñÂâáÔºåÂêàË≥áÊ†ºÊé•ÂèóË≥áÂä©ÔºöÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÂ∑¥ÊãøÂ∑¥ÊÑõÂøÉÊúçÂãôÂúòÔºàBarnabas Charitable Services AssociationÔºâ„ÄÅÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÂæóÁîüÂúòÂ•ëÔºàChristian New Being FellowshipÔºâ„ÄÅÂü∫Áù£Êïô‰ø°Áæ©ÊúÉËä¨Ëò≠Â∑ÆÊúÉÈùàÊÑõÈùíÂπ¥‰∏≠ÂøÉÔºàLing Oi Youth Centre, Finnish Evangelical Lutheran MissionÔºâ„ÄÅÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊô®Êõ¶ÊúÉÊô®Êõ¶Â≥∂ÊàíÊØí‰∏≠ÂøÉÔºàOperation Dawn‚Äôs Island CentreÔºâÂèäËÅñÂ£´ÊèêÂèçÊúÉÔºàSt. Stephen‚Äôs SocietyÔºâ„ÄÇ

              1998 Âπ¥3 ÊúàÔºåÁ§æÊúÉÁ¶èÂà©ÁΩ≤ÈñãÂßãË≥áÂä©4ÈñìÁÇ∫Êø´Áî®Ëó•Áâ©ËÄÖÊèê‰æõËá™È°ò‰ΩèÈô¢ÊàíÊØíË®àÂäÉÁöÑÈùûÊîøÂ∫úÊ©üÊßã„ÄÇ4 ÈñìÊ©üÊßãÂàÜÂà•ÁÇ∫Â∑¥ÊãøÂ∑¥ÊÑõÂøÉÊúçÂãôÂúò„ÄÅ‰ø°Áæ©ÊúÉËä¨Ëò≠Â∑ÆÊúÉÈùàÊÑõÈùíÂπ¥‰∏≠ÂøÉ„ÄÅÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊô®Êõ¶ÊúÉÂíåÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÂæóÁîüÂúòÂ•ëÔºàÁ†îÁ©∂ÂÆåÊàêÂæåÔºåÁî±ÊñºËÅñÂ£´ÊèêÂèçÊúÉÂ∑≤ÊâæÂà∞Á∂ìË≤ªËÄå‰∏çÁî≥Ë´ãË≥áÂä©ÔºåÔºâÔºåÊâÄÊèê‰æõÁöÑÊàíÊØíË®àÂäÉÁÇ∫ÊúüÈï∑Áü≠‰∏ç‰∏ÄÔºåÂÖßÂÆπÂêÑÁï∞Ôºå‰ΩÜÈÉΩÊòØ‰ª•Âü∫Áù£Êïô‰ø°‰ª∞ÁÇ∫Êú¨ÔºåÁùÄÈáçÂøÉÁêÜÁ§æÊúÉÂ∫∑Âæ©ËºîÂ∞éÔºå‰∏¶Ë®≠ÊúâÂñÑÂæåËºîÂ∞éÊúçÂãô„ÄÇ Âú®1998Ëá≥99Âπ¥Ë≤°ÊîøÂπ¥Â∫¶ÔºåÂêàÂÖ±Áç≤1600Â§öËê¨Ê∏ØÂÖÉÊí•Ê¨æ„ÄÇ

              1. Buddha‚Äôs Birthday Recognized as a Public Holiday for the First Time, 1999 (Buddhist Door, 2024)

                On May 22, 1999, Buddha‚Äôs Birthday was officially celebrated as a public holiday in Hong Kong for the first time. The Hong Kong Buddhist Association made a special request to the Chinese government and the Buddhist Association of China to bring the Buddha‚Äôs tooth relic, a national treasure from Lingguang Temple in Beijing, to Hong Kong. On that day, the Hong Kong Buddhist community held a grand celebration and veneration ceremony at the Hong Kong Coliseum, officiated by Elder Sik Kok-kwong. Prominent attendees included Zhao Puchu, Vice Chairman of the Chinese People‚Äôs Political Consultative Conference and President of the Buddhist Association of China, and Tung Chee-hwa, Chief Executive of the Hong Kong Special Administrative Region (HKSAR).

                As early as 1961, Sik Kok-kwong of the Hong Kong Buddhist Association had petitioned the Hong Kong government to recognize Buddha‚Äôs Birthday as a public holiday. The Director of Home Affairs expressed willingness to assist. The association launched a ‚Äú100,000 Signatures Campaign‚Äù to appeal to the government. Two years later, the association collected 120,000 signatures, which Sik Kok-kwong submitted to the Director of Home Affairs for forwarding to the Governor. While the government promised to consider the request, no decision was made for years.

                In 1985, Sik Kok-kwong, appointed as a member of the Basic Law Drafting Committee, raised the issue again during the committee‚Äôs discussions.

                In 1998, he renewed his appeal to the HKSAR government, submitting an official request, Application for Implementing Buddha‚Äôs Birthday as a Public Holiday in 1999, and a related petition to the Xinhua News Agency Hong Kong Branch. The petition emphasized that ‚ÄúHong Kong, under British rule for many years, had favored Western religious holidays, while holidays for religions predominantly followed by Chinese residents were largely absent, leading to a sense of discrimination. For equality, Buddha‚Äôs Birthday should be designated as a public holiday.‚Äù

                In 1998, the HKSAR government submitted the Holidays (Amendment) Ordinance 1998. A government spokesperson stated: ‚ÄúThe primary purpose of the ordinance is to designate Labor Day (May 1) and Buddha‚Äôs Birthday (the eighth day of the fourth lunar month) as public holidays starting in 1999, replacing the two public holidays observed in 1997 and 1998‚ÄîVictory Day of the Anti-Japanese War (the third Monday of August) and the day following National Day (October 2).‚Äù

                ‚Äî

                1. Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Ordinance, Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation Ordinance, and Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee Ordinance, 1999 (Press Release, 1999)

                  Following the establishment ceremony of the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui (Hong Kong Anglican Church Province) on October 25, 1998, the Secretary for Home Affairs signed a notice at the request of the church to enact the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Ordinance (Cap. 1157), the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee Ordinance (Cap. 1158), and the Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation Ordinance (Cap. 1159). These ordinances came into effect on February 26, 1999, and were gazetted the same day.

                  The Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Ordinance established the ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Province‚Äù as a corporate body under the name ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui‚Äù and provided provisions regarding its composition, powers, responsibilities, and related matters.

                  The Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation Ordinance provided for the continuation of the corporation formerly known as the ‚ÄúBishop of Victoria‚Äù under the name ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Foundation.‚Äù It outlined the foundation‚Äôs composition, powers, and responsibilities while repealing the Bishop of Victoria Incorporation Ordinance.

                  The Hong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee Ordinance ensured the continuation of the corporation formerly known as the ‚ÄúDiocesan Trusts Committee‚Äù under the name ‚ÄúHong Kong Sheng Kung Hui Property Management Committee.‚Äù It specified the committee‚Äôs composition, powers, and responsibilities, while repealing the Diocesan Trusts Committee Incorporation Ordinance.
